<p>Transportation and commerce are important factors in the state, mostly linked to importing and exporting through its four deepwater ports. The focus of most of these activities is the port of Veracruz. It has the most favored position on Mexico’s Gulf coast and is extensively used for exports to the United States, Latin America and Europe. Seventy-five percent of all port activity in Mexico takes place in Veracruz. The chief exports of this state are coffee, fresh fruits, fertilizer, sugar, fish and crustaceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schmal_15-27"><a href="#cite_note-schmal-15">[15]</a></sup> Other ports include those in Tuxpan and Coatzacoalcos. Most highway, rail and air connections link to the port of Veracruz and other ports to the south.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brittanica_14-7"><a href="#cite_note-brittanica-14">[14]</a></sup> The state has 73 companies that have been classified as high-volume exporters and it is ranked sixth in the country for exports. The state contains five major food wholesale markets, 146 government sponsored markets, about 75,000 private stores and 201 supermarkets. Wholesale vendors focus on agricultural products such as wood, livestock and food products. The major focal point for international business is the World Trade Center EXPOVER in Boca del Río. Inaugurated in 1989, the center has facilities to accommodate 5,000 people in 7,000 m2, an exhibition hall of 12,000m2, a business center and parking for over 800 vehicles.</p>
<p>In the industrial sector, relatively poor municipios are not catching up to relatively rich ones though the latter are not diverging either. A policy of encouraging much more domestic and/or foreign investment is called for if poorer areas are to prosper and the outflow of residents is to stop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Handcrafts">Handcrafts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Veracruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Handcrafts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the more rural and indigenous areas of the state, a number of handicrafts are still made and sold both to local buyers and to tourists. Many of these crafts are produced by communities that specialize in one or more types. Wood furniture and other items are made by the Huasteca people, mostly using cedar and palm trees. The best work comes from the towns of Ozulama and Castillo de Teayo. Teocelo and Monte Blanco are known for bamboo furniture and other items. Musical instruments of wood such as a guitar called “jarana” are constructed in the Los Tuxtlas area, especially in Catemaco, with flutes made in Papantla. Wooden masks are made in Teocelo, and items made with the wood of coffee plants are made in Misantla, Coatepec, Huastusco and Xico. Corn husks are used to make decorative figures, often religious, in Nautla and Naranjos de Amatlán. Palm fronds are woven into fans, shoes and baskets in Jalcomulco, Ozulama and Tlalixcoyan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones4042_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-rincones4042-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ceramics have been made in almost all parts of Veracruz since the Olmecs. One area known for its work is Papantla which also includes life sized representations of folk dancers from the area along with more mundane items of glazed and unglazed pottery. Minatitlán is known for its production of ceramic cooking utensils which are also popular in the neighboring municipalities of Actopan and Naolinco. San Miguel Aguasuelos and Jalcomulco are known for their white clay wares which include water jars, toys, nativity scenes, bells and more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones4042_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-rincones4042-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>Traditional clothing and embroidery can be most easily found in the La Huasteca area, where elaborately decorated women's blouses can be seen, especially in the El Higo and Tlalixcoyan area. In Totonacalpan, men are still often seen in white shirts and pants with a bag to hold personal items. This dress dates back to the early colonial period and had not changed much since then. Other areas specialize in wool items such as Naranjos de Amatlán, Minatilán and the city of Veracruz where items such as dresses, skirts and jackets. These and other textils such as tablecloths and napkins are often decorated with cross-stitch. Leather items include shoes, jackets, bags, wallets, belts and boots and are usually made in the La Huasteca region, Teocolo, Citlaltépetl and Naolinco.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones4042_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-rincones4042-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Veracruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gastronomy">Gastronomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Veracruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Gastronomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HuachinangoVeracruzana.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/HuachinangoVeracruzana.JPG/220px-HuachinangoVeracruzana.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/HuachinangoVeracruzana.JPG/330px-HuachinangoVeracruzana.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/HuachinangoVeracruzana.JPG/440px-HuachinangoVeracruzana.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HuachinangoVeracruzana.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Huachinango (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_snapper_(fish)" title="Red snapper (fish)">red snapper</a>) a la Veracruzana</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The gastronomy of the state is unique in Mexico and mixed Spanish, indigenous, and other influences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup> From the pre-Hispanic period, the cuisine of the state was unique. The staple triumvirate of corn, beans, and squash was supplemented by tropical fruits, vanilla beans, and an herb called acuyo or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hoja_santa" title="Hoja santa">hoja santa</a>. Another important native contribution is <a href="/wiki/Seafood" title="Seafood">seafood</a>, which is featured in many dishes such as, <a href="/wiki/Arroz_a_la_tumbada" title="Arroz a la tumbada">arroz a la tumbada</a> and caldo de mariscos (seafood soup).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-khursh_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-khursh-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the conquest and during the colonial period, many other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spices" title="Spices">spices</a> and ingredients were brought and have had a greater influence in the cooking here than in other parts of the country. From Europe, the Spanish brought <a href="/wiki/Saffron" title="Saffron">saffron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parsley" title="Parsley">parsley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thyme" title="Thyme">thyme</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marjoram" title="Marjoram">marjoram</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bay_laurel" title="Bay laurel">bay laurel</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cilantro" title="Cilantro">cilantro</a> as well Asian spices such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cloves" title="Cloves">cloves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cinnamon" title="Cinnamon">cinnamon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Black_pepper" title="Black pepper">black pepper</a>. The Spaniards also brought wheat, rice, almonds, olives and olive oil, garlic, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capers" title="Capers">capers</a>. The latter three are essential ingredients in what is perhaps the most famous specialty of the region, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huachinango_a_la_veracruzana" title="Huachinango a la veracruzana">huachinango a la veracruzana</a>, red snapper in a spicy tomato sauce. Caribbean imports such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sugar_cane" title="Sugar cane">sugar cane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pineapple" title="Pineapple">pineapple</a> were adapted as well as the <a href="/wiki/Peanut" title="Peanut">peanut</a>, brought from Africa by the Portuguese via Brazil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-khursh_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-khursh-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Veracruz cuisine divides into six regions called Sotavento, Centro Norte, Centro Sur, Sierra, Costa Norte, and Los Tuxtlas. The Sotovento area is in the south of the state, and the dishes here are heavily based on <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a>. Common dishes include arroz a la tumbada, which is rice cooked with seafood or meat and rice with fried bananas. Seafood dishes are also prominent based mostly on fish and shrimp. A common ingredient in dishes is a herb called “hoja santa” or “hierba Santa,” which is a plant of the <a href="/wiki/Piperaceae" title="Piperaceae">Piperaceae</a> family. The Centro Norte is centered on Xalapa. Dishes here tend to be more indigenous in nature, heavily flavored with mild chili peppers. Common dishes here include Chilehuates, similar to a tamale, stuffed chile peppers, and enchiladas. Less seafood and more pork and domestic fowl are consumed. The Centro Sur area is mostly indigenous and encompasses the area of, Huatusco, Coscomatepec, Cotaxtla, Orizaba, Amatlán, Huilango y las españolas Córdoba y Fortín de las Flores, la negra Yanga and San Lorenzo de los Negros. Dishes here are similar to Centro Norte, but <a href="/wiki/Chayote" title="Chayote">chayotes</a> appear more often as this region is a major producer of the vegetable. Meats in <a href="/wiki/Adobo" title="Adobo">adobo</a> sauce are common as well. The Sierra and Costa Norte encompass the northern part of the state, such as the Pánuco River area and Totonacapan. This area is noted for a number of unique dishes such as frijoles en achuchutl, made with black beans, pork rind, <a href="/wiki/Chayote" title="Chayote">chayotes</a>, squash seeds, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jalape%C3%B1o_pepper" title="Jalapeño pepper">jalapeño peppers</a>. <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Bocole&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Bocole (page does not exist)">Bocoles</a> are a kind of filled tortilla made with corn dough, stuffed with black beans, chorizo, eggs, or seafood, which then are fried in lard. Tamales are often made with banana leaves. The area is also known for its breads, especially <a href="/wiki/Anise" title="Anise">anise</a>–flavored rolls. The Los Tuxtlas area is centered on the communities of Santiago, San Andrés and Catemaco, which were the center of the Olmec civilization. The cuisine in this area features yucca, “chocos” (a type edible flower), fish, especially mojarra, and exotic meats such as monkey, and <a href="/wiki/Iguana" title="Iguana">iguana</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Museums">Museums</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Veracruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Museums">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:San_Martin_Pajapan_Monument_1_retouched.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1548" data-file-width="1080" height="430" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/San_Martin_Pajapan_Monument_1_retouched.jpg/300px-San_Martin_Pajapan_Monument_1_retouched.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/San_Martin_Pajapan_Monument_1_retouched.jpg/450px-San_Martin_Pajapan_Monument_1_retouched.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/San_Martin_Pajapan_Monument_1_retouched.jpg/600px-San_Martin_Pajapan_Monument_1_retouched.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:San_Martin_Pajapan_Monument_1_retouched.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Olmec <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/San_Martin_Pajapan_Monument_1" title="San Martin Pajapan Monument 1">San Martin Pajapan Monument 1</a> on exhibit in the Museum of Anthropology in Xalapa</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The state capital of Xalapa is also home to a number of important museums. The Museum of Anthropology contains the second most important collection of Mesoamerican artifacts in the country. It was built beginning in 1959 over six hectares. The complex is divided into to various halls and galleries by theme, focusing on the Olmec and Totonac cultures. The Patio Olmeca contains the colossal head found in 1945 and known as El Rey (The King). Other important artifacts include giant stelae and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/San_Martin_Pajapan_Monument_1" title="San Martin Pajapan Monument 1">San Martin Pajapan Monument 1</a> (at right). The Museum of Science and Technology is in Xalapa. It contains more than 400 exhibitions in eight halls: Life, Ecology, Space, Transportation, Sciences, Energy, Water and Earth. The Pinoteca Diega Rivera was established by the state in the former Monastery of San Francisco in Xalapa. Its primary function is to preserve and display <a href="/wiki/Diego_Rivera" title="Diego Rivera">Rivera</a>’s works owned by the state, but it also contains early works by <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Mar%C3%ADa_Velasco_G%C3%B3mez" title="José María Velasco Gómez">José María Velasco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jorge_Cuesta" title="Jorge Cuesta">Jorge Cuesta</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Teodoro_A._Dehesa&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Teodoro A. Dehesa (page does not exist)">Teodoro A. Dehesa</a>. Near the city is the Hacienda del Lencero, which was the home and headquarters of President Antonio López de Santa Anna in the 19th century. It has been preserved and turned into a museum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Tuxpan is the Regional Museum of Anthropology with more than 400 pieces from pre-Hispanic groups in the region, displayed in four halls. Most of the pieces come from the center of the state and from the Huasteca region.On the edge of the Tuxpan River.The city also has the Mexican-Cuban Museum. It contains a collection of photographs from the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Revolution" title="Cuban Revolution">Cuban Revolution</a> as well as a statue of <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Mart%C3%AD" title="José Martí">José Martí</a> .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-3"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other museums are scattered in other parts of the state. The <a href="/wiki/Salvador_Ferrando" title="Salvador Ferrando">Salvador Ferrando</a> Museum is located in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tlacotalpan,_Veracruz" title="Tlacotalpan, Veracruz">Tlacotalpan</a> and contains many everyday items and art from the 16th to the 19th centuries.The Tuxteco Regional Museum in Santiago de Tuxtla contains Olmec and Totonac artifacts including art objects, farming implements, utensils and more. In the garden area, there are giant stone sculptures from the Tres Zapotes site. The Jardín Central (Central Garden) of the municipality of <a href="/wiki/Tierra_Blanca,_Veracruz" title="Tierra Blanca, Veracruz">Tierra Blanca</a> has a number of Totonac archeological pieces on display. The Archeological Museum of Córdoba is in the city of the same name with three exhibition halls containing artifacts from the Maya, Olmec, Toltec and Huastec cultures. There is also a collection of historical photographs of the city. The Veracruz State Art Museum (Museo de Arte del Estado de Veracruz) is located in Orizaba in what was the monastery associated with the Concordia Church. Its collection includes works by Diego Rivera, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ignacio_Rosas&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ignacio Rosas (page does not exist)">Ignacio Rosas</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Gonzalo_Arg%C3%BCelles&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Gonzalo Argüelles (page does not exist)">Gonzalo Argüelles</a>. It also contains a collection of historical photographs related to Veracruz and art. The Malintzin Archeological Museum is in the municipality of <a href="/wiki/Nogales,_Veracruz" title="Nogales, Veracruz">Nogales</a>. It is a small museum with one hall, with photographs and documents. It is located in the church where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malinche" title="Malinche">Malinche</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Juan_de_Jaramillo&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Juan de Jaramillo (page does not exist)">Juan de Jaramillo</a> were supposedly married.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-4"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fairs_and_festivals">Fairs and festivals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Veracruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Fairs and festivals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The state is noted for its quantity and variety of festivals. The most important of these is <a href="/wiki/Carnival" title="Carnival">Carnival</a> in the city of Veracruz. This city’s version of the event begins with the “burning of bad humor,” which is represented in effigy. A number of kings and queens are "crowned" including categories for children but the most important is the Rey Feo (Ugly King) and the Reina del Carnaval (Queen of the Carnival). The latter is accompanies by cadets from the Naval Academy during the parade. This celebration is repeated all along the Veracruz coastline with other significant festivities taking place in <a href="/wiki/Alvarado,_Veracruz" title="Alvarado, Veracruz">Alvarado</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coatzacoalcos" title="Coatzacoalcos">Coatzacoalcos</a>, <a href="/wiki/San_Rafael,_Veracruz" title="San Rafael, Veracruz">San Rafael</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Cardel,_Veracruz" title="José Cardel, Veracruz">Villa José Cardel</a>. <a href="/wiki/Minatitl%C3%A1n,_Veracruz" title="Minatitlán, Veracruz">Minatitlán</a>'s celebration draws people from the nearby states of Oaxaca and Tabasco.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones23_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-rincones23-36">[36]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Day_of_the_Dead" title="Day of the Dead">Day of the Dead</a> is celebrated in almost all of Mexico from 31 Oct to 2 November but there are local twists in the state. In some places, it is commemorated during the months of August and September. In Papantla, boards or tables are placed on rooftops, which have been adorned with flowers, plant matter and more. In Tantoyuca, it is commemorated with costumes and music, similar to Carnival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-5"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Christian celebration of <a href="/wiki/Candlemas" title="Candlemas">Candlemas</a> is fused with traditions associated with <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Chalchiuhtlicua&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Chalchiuhtlicua (page does not exist)">Chalchiuhtlicua</a>, the goddess of water, rivers, lakes and ocean. She was replaced by the Virgin of Candlemas, the protector of fishermen, making this celebration particularly important on the coast, especially in Tlacotalpan, where it is celebrated with much pomp. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J%C3%A1ltipan_de_Morelos" title="Jáltipan de Morelos">Jáltipan de Morelos</a>, ethnic <a href="/wiki/Nahua_peoples" title="Nahua peoples">Nahuas</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Popolucas&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Popolucas (page does not exist)">Popolucas</a> dress in elaborate costumes and arrange their hair in intricate styles. In Santa María Magdalena, on 22 July, bulls are set free to roam the streets. Corn harvest festivals are prominent in the Huasteca region in municipalities such as <a href="/wiki/Chontla" title="Chontla">Chontla</a>, Chicontepec, and <a href="/wiki/Ixhuatl%C3%A1n_de_Madero" title="Ixhuatlán de Madero">Ixhuatlán de Madero</a>. These generally include native dances and foods based on corn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-6"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones23_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-rincones23-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dance_and_music">Dance and music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Veracruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Dance and music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="mbox-small-left" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;float:left">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div class="center">

<tr>
<td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em"><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The state is well known in the country for its music and dance. The <a href="/wiki/Fandango" title="Fandango">fandango</a> is a dance brought over from Spanish. Today the state has two varieties: the jarocho and the huasteco.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-7"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup> Indigenous and folk dances in the state are most often associated with rituals and religious festivals. These include one called Los Lisceres also called the Tigres from the Los Tuxtlas region. Participants wear Olmec style masks which represent the rain god Tlalóc. Another is Los Guaguas in which the participants pay homage to the sun, and Los Santiagos, which is related to the veneration of Saint James, patron saint of Hernán Cortés. One last one is called Los Negritos (The Little Black Ones) which was created by African slaves. According to tradition, the origin of this dance lies in a story about a boy who was bitten by a snake and the rituals his family held to heal him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones22_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-rincones22-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, the state’s most famous dance is as much a ritual and daredevil act as movements performed to music. The <b>Danza de los Voladores</b> (Dance of the Flyers) is a ceremony/ritual which has its roots in the pre-Hispanic period and presently best known as associated with the town of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Papantla,_Veracruz" title="Papantla, Veracruz">Papantla</a>, Veracruz. It is believed to have originated with the <a href="/wiki/Nahua_peoples" title="Nahua peoples">Nahua</a>, <a href="/wiki/Huastec_people" title="Huastec people">Huastec</a> and <a href="/wiki/Otomi_people" title="Otomi people">Otomi</a> peoples in central Mexico, and then spread throughout most of <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</a>. The ritual consists of dance and the climbing of a 30-meter pole from which four of the five participants then launch themselves tied with ropes to descend to the ground. The fifth remains on top of the pole, dancing and playing a flute and drum. According to myth, the ritual was created to ask the gods to end a severe <a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">drought</a>. Although the ritual did not originate with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Totonac" title="Totonac">Totonac</a> people, today it is most strongly associated with them, especially those in and around <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Papantla,_Veracruz" title="Papantla, Veracruz">Papantla</a>, as the ceremony has died off in most other places.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilkerson7576_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-wilkerson7576-38">[38]</a></sup> The ceremony was named an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intangible_Cultural_Heritage" title="Intangible Cultural Heritage">Intangible Cultural Heritage</a> by <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> in order to help the ritual survive and thrive in the modern world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcclat_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-mcclat-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>The state's best-known musical style is called the "son". A “<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Son_(music)" title="Son (music)">son</a>” is a musical variation which traces its origins to Spain and developed during the 17th and 18th centuries. It is the state’s most popular musical style shows influences from the many peoples who have lived here such as indigenous groups, Portuguese, Italians, Africans, French and others. The music is generally performed by harps, violins and guitars, with an occasional wind instrument. <a href="/wiki/Huapango" title="Huapango">Son huasteco (also called son huapango)</a> is a variety of son played in the north of the state mostly among the Totonacs. Son jarocho is the better known and more popular variety played in the south of the state. The famous Grammy award-winning song "La Bamba" by <a href="/wiki/Los_Lobos" title="Los Lobos">Los Lobos</a> is said have its roots in a traditional folk song from Veracruz (hence the reference to the "Marinero" in that song).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-8"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones2021_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-rincones2021-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>The state has produced a number of musicians famous in the country. One of the best known is Francisco Gabilondo Soler. Gabilondo Soler is best known for creating a character known as “Cri-cri”, a singing cricket for a radio show in the first half of the 20th century. As a musician, he specialized in writing children’s songs such as "Abuelito", "Caminito de la escuela", "El baile de los muñecos", "El burrito" and "La negrita Cucurumbé". A number of his works have been translated into other languages. Another famous musician is Agustín Lara, who has had more international fame. Nicknamed "Flaco de oro" (golden skinny one), he always insisted that he was born in Tlacotalpan, Veracruz and not Mexico City as records show. Lara formed his first band in 1930 called El Son de Marabú and toured almost continuously in Mexico and abroad during his career. His most famous compositions include "Veracruz", "Noche de Ronda" and "Solamente una vez".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones2021_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-rincones2021-40">[40]</a></sup> Other prominent musicians include Toña "La Negra" or María Antonia del Carmen Peregino, Narcisco Serradel, Lorenzo Barcelata and María Greever.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-9"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art_and_architecture">Art and architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Veracruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Art and architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MuralChurchCanoPap.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/MuralChurchCanoPap.JPG/220px-MuralChurchCanoPap.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/MuralChurchCanoPap.JPG/330px-MuralChurchCanoPap.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/MuralChurchCanoPap.JPG/440px-MuralChurchCanoPap.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MuralChurchCanoPap.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mural depicting the history of Papantla in the town square by Teodoro Cano García</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the colonial era, a movement called the “estilo veracruzano” (Veracruz style) developed mostly focusing on landscapes in the state with a certain amount of indigenous influence although the painters themselves were <i>criollo</i> or Mexico-born Spanish. These paintings focus on the mountains, valles, coasts, volcanos and other natural phenomena in the state. Most of the proponents of this were born in Veracruz itself and include <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jos%C3%A9_Justo_Montiel&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="José Justo Montiel (page does not exist)">José Justo Montiel</a> (1824–1899), <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Gonzalo_Arg%C3%BCelles_Bringas&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Gonzalo Argüelles Bringas (page does not exist)">Gonzalo Argüelles Bringas</a> (1877–1942), <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Eugenio_Landecio&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Eugenio Landecio (page does not exist)">Eugenio Landecio</a> (teacher of <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Mar%C3%ADa_Velasco_G%C3%B3mez" title="José María Velasco Gómez">José María Velasco</a>), <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Natal_Pesado&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Natal Pesado (page does not exist)">Natal Pesado</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ignacio_Rosas&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ignacio Rosas (page does not exist)">Ignacio Rosas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones1215_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-rincones1215-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, most of Veracruz’s best-known artists are from the 19th and 20th centuries. In the 19th century, these include <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Miguel_Mata_Reyes&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Miguel Mata Reyes (page does not exist)">Miguel Mata Reyes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salvador_Ferrando" title="Salvador Ferrando">Salvador Ferrando</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Mar%C3%ADa_Jara" title="José María Jara">José María Jara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Enrique_Guerra" title="Enrique Guerra">Enrique Guerra</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Alberto_Fuster&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Alberto Fuster (page does not exist)">Alberto Fuster</a>. Miguel Mata Reyes is best known for his contributions to the design of the Palacio de Bellas Artes as well a portrait of <a href="/wiki/Antonio_L%C3%B3pez_de_Santa_Anna" title="Antonio López de Santa Anna">Antonio López de Santa Anna</a>. Salvador Ferrando was a portrait and landscape artist from the north of the state. Until recently, most of his work had been hidden in a museum named after him in the Tlacotalpan region. Much of it now is on display at the Museo de Arte de Veracruz in Orizaba. José María Jara is noted for his paintings of Veracruz customs, whose works include <i>El Velorio</i>, which was presented at the World’s Fair in Paris. Enrique Guerra was an important sculptor at the end of the 19th century. His best-known works are bas reliefs and include <i>Asesinato de César</i>, <i>Coroliano</i>, <i>Thais</i> and <i>Crisálida</i>. Alberto Fuster was most active at the end of the century and is noted for bringing symbolism painting to Mexico from his stay in Europe. His works include <i>El progreso</i>, <i>Safo en el templo de Delfos</i> and <i>Nativa con loro</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones1215_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-rincones1215-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are three important artists from the 20th century, <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Bracho" title="Carlos Bracho">Carlos Bracho</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Norberto_Mart%C3%ADnez&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Norberto Martínez (page does not exist)">Norberto Martínez</a> and <a href="/wiki/Teodoro_Cano_Garc%C3%ADa" title="Teodoro Cano García">Teodoro Cano García</a>. Active in the first half of the century, sculptor Carlos Bracho’s work has been compared to that of <a href="/wiki/Juan_Rulfo" title="Juan Rulfo">Juan Rulfo</a>. His works have been done in plaster, bronze, terracotta and green onyx and include monumental works which can be found in the cities of Xalapa, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puebla,_Puebla" title="Puebla, Puebla">Puebla</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pachuca" title="Pachuca">Pachuca</a> and Mexico City. His best-known works are El abrazo, Cabeza verde and El campesino se apodera de la tierra. Norberto Martínez only lived 45 years but is considered one of the most prolific of Mexican painters who dedicated most of his works to social themes. A number of these are early murals such as El comercio in the Jáuregi de Xalapa market as well as an untitled work in a private home in Córdoba which deals with the fusion of the Spanish, indigenous and African races in Mexico. Later works include the three murals in the main stairwell of the School of Law at the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_Xalapa&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="University of Xalapa (page does not exist)">University of Xalapa</a> and El hombre y el conocimiento at the <a href="/wiki/Universidad_Veracruzana" title="Universidad Veracruzana">Universidad Veracruzana</a>. Teodoro Cano García is one of Mexico’s most famous muralists of the late 20th century, famous for the promotion of the <a href="/wiki/Totonac_people" title="Totonac people">Totonac</a> culture of his hometown of Papantla. He has created paintings, sculptures, etchings, photography and mixed media works with his murals and sculptures most acclaimed. Examples of his work can be seen in various parts of public buildings in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Papantla,_Veracruz" title="Papantla, Veracruz">Papantla</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones1215_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-rincones1215-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most of Veracruz’s older architecture can be found in the inland cities of Xalapa and Córdoba. Despite being the first Spanish settlement, the city of Veracruz lost most its older structures to the various invasions it has suffered. Architecture from the 16th to the 19th century includes colonial Spanish, Moorish, Neo gothic and Neoclassical. From the 20th century on, a number of names stand out. Armando Bravo Ramírez remodeled the State Government Palace and the facade of the Capillas de Ánimas, both in Xalapa. Other prominent names responsible for many projects in the state include Luis González Aparicio, Bernal Lascuraín Rangel and Luis Manuel Tello Deschamps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones1215_41-3"><a href="#cite_note-rincones1215-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Veracruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The literary arts reached their peak in Veracruz starting in the 19th century and extends to the “Generation of the 1950s.” <a href="/wiki/Salvador_D%C3%ADaz_Mir%C3%B3n" title="Salvador Díaz Mirón">Salvador Díaz Mirón</a> is one of Veracruz’s most-distinguished poets. Over his lifetime from the latter 19th to early 20th centuries, he worked as a professor, politician and journalist contributing to periodicals such as El Veracruzano, El Orden, and El Imparcial. His creative works include some of the first Romantic pieces produced in Mexico such as Oda a Víctor Hugo, Ojos verdes, Gloria and Voces interiores. Other works include <i>Poesías A Tirsa</i>, <i>Nox</i> (also known as <i>Claudia</i>) and his last works such as <i>Al buen cura</i> and <i>La mujer de nieve</i>. He became a member of the <a href="/wiki/Academia_Mexicana_de_la_Lengua" title="Academia Mexicana de la Lengua">Academia Mexicana de la Lengua</a> and is buried at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rotonda_de_los_Hombres_Ilustres" title="Rotonda de los Hombres Ilustres">Rotonda de los Hombres Ilustres</a> in Mexico City. <a href="/wiki/Mar%C3%ADa_Enriqueta_Camarillo" title="María Enriqueta Camarillo">María Enriqueta Camarillo</a> was one of a number of women writers to gain prominence in Mexico at the end of the 19th century. While she wrote a number of works such as <i>Jirón del mundo</i>, <i>Sorpresas de la vida</i> and <i>El Secreto</i>, she is best known for <i>Rosas de la infancia</i>, with which many Mexicans learned to read.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones1618_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-rincones1618-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Writers born at the end of the 19th century, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gregorio_L%C3%B3pez_y_Fuentes" title="Gregorio López y Fuentes">Gregorio López y Fuentes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Maples_Arce" title="Manuel Maples Arce">Manuel Maples Arce</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jorge_Mateo_Cuesta_Porte-Petit&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jorge Mateo Cuesta Porte-Petit (page does not exist)">Jorge Mateo Cuesta Porte-Petit</a> were often concerned with social issues. Works of this type include El Indio by López, Metrópolis by Maples and a poem called <i>Canto a un dios mineral</i> by Cuesta. The following generation, born in the first decades of the 20th century, became known as the Generation of the 1950s. During this time Veracruz’s literary tradition consolidated and decided to break type. One example is <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Juan_Hern%C3%A1ndez_Ram%C3%ADrez&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Juan Hernández Ramírez (page does not exist)">Juan Hernández Ramírez</a>’s writing of poetry in the <a href="/wiki/Nahuatl" title="Nahuatl">Nahuatl</a> spoken in the <a href="/wiki/La_Huasteca" title="La Huasteca">La Huasteca</a> area of Veracruz. One important name from this generation is <a href="/wiki/Emilio_Carballido" title="Emilio Carballido">Emilio Carballido</a> who wrote about 100 plays as well as scripts for radio and television. Some of his works include <i>Rosalba y los llaveros</i>, <i>Felicidad</i> and <i>Las visitaciones del Diablo</i>. In 1996, he won the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Prize_of_Arts_and_Sciences_(Mexico)" title="National Prize of Arts and Sciences (Mexico)">Premio Nacional de Literatura</a> and in 2002 he received the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ariel_de_Oro" title="Ariel de Oro">Ariel de Oro</a> for his work in cinema.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones1618_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-rincones1618-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Veracruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The patron saint of Veracruz is <a href="/wiki/Rafael_Gu%C3%ADzar_y_Valencia" title="Rafael Guízar y Valencia">Rafael Guízar y Valencia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Veracruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Public education in the state is supervised by the state Direción General de Educación Popular and the Dirección General de Educación Media Superior y Superior. The current system is the result of a number of reforms which took place in the 1980s and 1990s. In the late 1990s, 302 new school campuses were created statewide and 257 schools were remodeled. These included new schools for special education, distance learning and technological institutes, giving the state one of the highest number of school campuses in the country. There are a total of 20,479 schools, with nearly 2 million students and about 85,000 teachers. 93% of schools are in the basic education category (preschool, primary and middle schools. Preschools also include those geared towards the indigenous populations, focusing on bilingual and bicultural education in both the indigenous language/culture and Spanish. One major focus of these and other schools is to eliminate illiteracy in indigenous communities. The "Medio Superior" level includes vocational high school and technical colleges. These account for 6.6% of schools in the public system. The Superior level includes teachers’ colleges and universities. There are 166 institutes at this level, with about 68,000 students studying 221 different majors. There are also 63 master’s degree programs and six PhDs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-infraes_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-infraes-44">[44]</a></sup> These institutions serve about 135,000 students accounting for about 19% of the college-aged population (19- to 24-year-olds), slightly below the national average of 24%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OECD_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-OECD-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>The major state university is the <a href="/wiki/Universidad_Veracruzana" title="Universidad Veracruzana">Universidad Veracruzana</a>, with offers 56 bachelor's degrees, 37 masters and 5 PhDs. It is based in the capital of Xalapa and is noted for its large and varied sports programs. There are campuses in fourteen other cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-infraes_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-infraes-44">[44]</a></sup> About 37% of university students attend the main public university, with a student population of 47,000 undergraduates and 2,000 postgraduates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OECD_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-OECD-45">[45]</a></sup> Other important schools include Instituto Tecnologico de Veracruz in Veracruz, Universidad Anáhuac in Xalapa, Universidad de Xalapa in Xalapa, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ITESM" title="ITESM">ITESM</a> in Córdoba, Universidad Cristóbal Colón in Veracruz, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Veracruz_Naval_Academy" title="Veracruz Naval Academy">Veracruz Naval Academy</a> and the Instituto Tecnológico del Mar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-infraes_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-infraes-44">[44]</a></sup></p>

<p>The state of Veracruz, especially its port, has been a crossroads for various cultures since the very early colonial period. The port of Veracruz has brought cargo, sailors, seamen, and slaves from various parts of the world, especially from the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a> and Europe. The state has indigenous cultural influences mixed with those from Europe, Africa and the <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Afro-Caribbean" title="wikt:Afro-Caribbean">Afro-Caribbean</a>. These can be best seen in the music, the culinary traditions and in the people themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-khursh_11-4"><a href="#cite_note-khursh-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>The number of ethnic communities in the state has been calculated at 2,062. The most numerous include the <a href="/wiki/Nahua_peoples" title="Nahua peoples">Nahuas</a>, Totonacs, Huastecs, Popolucs, <a href="/wiki/Zapotec_peoples" title="Zapotec peoples">Zapotecas</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Chinanteca_(people)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Chinanteca (people) (page does not exist)">Chinantecas</a>, Otomis, Mazatecas, Tepehuas, Mixtecas, Zoques, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mixe" title="Mixe">Mixes</a>, Mayas and <a href="/wiki/Tzotzil_people" title="Tzotzil people">Tzotzils</a>, all indigenous groups. The largest are Nahuas, who make up over half of the indigenous population. Most native communities can be found in 68 municipalities especially in <a href="/wiki/Tehuipango" title="Tehuipango">Tehuipango</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mixtla_de_Altamirano" title="Mixtla de Altamirano">Mixtla de Altamirano</a>, <a href="/wiki/Astacinga" title="Astacinga">Astacinga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soledad_Atzompa" title="Soledad Atzompa">Soledad Atzompa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atlahuilco" title="Atlahuilco">Atlahuilco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tequila,_Veracruz" title="Tequila, Veracruz">Tequila</a>, Tlaquilpan, Los Reyes, Magdalena, San Andres Tenejapan, Tantoyuca, Zongolica, Chicontepec, Papantla, Ixhuatlán de Madero, Soteapan, Playa Vicente, Mecayapan y Coyutla, Benito Juárez, Coxquihi, Espinal, Filomeno Mata, Ixcatepec, Mecatlán and Zozocolco de Hidalgo. In 1998, about 10% of the population spoke an indigenous language; however, this does not take into account all indigenous peoples. The census of 2005 counted 605,135 as speaking an indigenous language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perfilsoc_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-perfilsoc-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are also small immigrant communities of Spaniards, Italians, Basque and <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_people" title="Lebanese people">Lebanese</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-10"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup> African slaves were imported to Mexico through the Veracruz port. At one point, they outnumbered Europeans and a significant number ran away from haciendas and plantations to form their own communities, sometimes allied with indigenous groups. One such rebellion was led by Yanga, who successfully negotiated a free African community with Spanish authorities in 1609. Like other groups, many of African descent would intermarry with other groups, with the category of “mulatto” existing in the old colonial caste system for those with African blood. Today, the vast majority of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afro_Mexican" title="Afro Mexican">Afro Mexicans</a> in Veracruz and other parts of the country are spread out and intermixed with the rest of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-africanroots_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-africanroots-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>With a population of 7,110,214 (2005), Veracruz is the third most populous entity in the country, after the Federal District of Mexico City and the <a href="/wiki/State_of_Mexico" title="State of Mexico">State of Mexico</a>. Population growth has slowed in the state in the last decades, due to lower birthrates and the exodus of migrants, mostly men. Women outnumber men. One reason for the decline in birthrates is the elevation of education levels, especially among women. Another is urbanization, with about one-third of the state’s population living in urban centers, especially Veracruz, Xalapa, Coatzacoalcos, Minatitlán and Papantla. Most (90%) of the state’s communities, outside of municipal seats have fewer than 500 people and contain only 21% of the total population. The migration of men outside the state has put more women into the state's workforce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perfilsoc_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-perfilsoc-48">[48]</a></sup> Approximately 75% of the population is under 45 years of age and 30% under the age of 15.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-habitantes_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-habitantes-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:178px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leuchtturm_Veracruz_fcm.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2560" data-file-width="1920" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Leuchtturm_Veracruz_fcm.jpg/176px-Leuchtturm_Veracruz_fcm.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Leuchtturm_Veracruz_fcm.jpg/264px-Leuchtturm_Veracruz_fcm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Leuchtturm_Veracruz_fcm.jpg/352px-Leuchtturm_Veracruz_fcm.jpg 2x" width="176"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leuchtturm_Veracruz_fcm.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Veracruz lighthouse</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Life expectancy is just under the norm for the rest of the country. The overwhelming majority of people in the state are Catholic, however, there is a significant Protestant minority and a number who profess the Jewish faith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perfilsoc_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-perfilsoc-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Veracruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Tourism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Tourism mostly centers on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Port_city" title="Port city">port city</a> of Veracruz but there are other destinations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-11"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup> There are over 1,000 hotels in the state, over half of which are small, family-owned enterprises. Almost all of the four and five-star establishments are in metropolitan area of Veracruz. Many of the state major historical and cultural monuments are located in the port of Veracruz. Some of these include the Aquarium, the Museum of the City (Museo de la Ciudad), The Agustín Lara Museum, the Santiago Fortress (Baluarte de Santiago) the "Las Atarazanas" Museum and the San Juan de Ulúa Fort.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-12"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>To the north of the port city is the Sierra or Totonacalpan area of the state, home to the Totonac people. This is home to the important pre-Hispanic city of <a href="/wiki/El_Taj%C3%ADn" title="El Tajín">El Tajín</a> and the present-day city of <a href="/wiki/Papantla" title="Papantla">Papantla</a>. The modern city is best known as the home of the Totonac version of the “<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Danza_de_los_Voladores_de_Papantla" title="Danza de los Voladores de Papantla">danza de voladores</a>”; there, the dancers spin from 80 ft (24.4 m) high poles. The area is also the native habitat of the vanilla bean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-khursh_11-5"><a href="#cite_note-khursh-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>To the south of the port is on the coast, is Catemaco. This is in a tropical area. The area’s two main features are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lake_Catemaco" title="Lake Catemaco">Lake Catemaco</a>, which is located in the crater of an extinct volcano and Isla Tanaxpillo just off the coast. This island is also called the island of the monkeys or baboons due to a group of feral monkeys that escaped and found refuge here.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-khursh_11-6"><a href="#cite_note-khursh-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-13"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>Inland is the coffee-growing region in and around the cities of Coatepec and Xalapa. Orizaba is best known for the volcano nearby but also has a large waterfall called El Elefante and a Cañon (Canyon) de Río Blanco.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-14"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Archeological_sites">Archeological sites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Veracruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Archeological sites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The state contains numerous remains of pre-Hispanic <a href="/wiki/Olmec" title="Olmec">Olmec</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Totonac" title="Totonac">Totonac</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Huastec_civilization" title="Huastec civilization">Huastec</a> cities. El Tajín, a ruined city that reached its apex between the 9th and 13th centuries ad, was designated a UNESCO World Heritage site in 1992.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brittanica_14-8"><a href="#cite_note-brittanica-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>El Zapotal is an archeological site which was discovered in 1971 in a region known at Mixtequlla (between the Blanco and Papaloapan Rivers). This site is noted for its clay figurines with smiling faces, part of an extremely large offering in honor of the god of death <a href="/wiki/Mictlantecuhtli" title="Mictlantecuhtli">Mictlantecuhtli</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-15"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>Cempoala is an archeological site located on the coast between the modern settlements of La Antigua and Ciudad Cardel. It was occupied when Hernán Cortés arrived, and he managed to form an alliance with the Totonacs here against the Aztecs. In the center of the site, there is a large plaza surrounded by temples and the palace of the Totonac chief. The site also has a small museum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-16"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:El_Taj%C3%ADn,_Nischenpyramide.fcm.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/El_Taj%C3%ADn%2C_Nischenpyramide.fcm.jpg/230px-El_Taj%C3%ADn%2C_Nischenpyramide.fcm.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/El_Taj%C3%ADn%2C_Nischenpyramide.fcm.jpg/345px-El_Taj%C3%ADn%2C_Nischenpyramide.fcm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/El_Taj%C3%ADn%2C_Nischenpyramide.fcm.jpg/460px-El_Taj%C3%ADn%2C_Nischenpyramide.fcm.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:El_Taj%C3%ADn,_Nischenpyramide.fcm.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
El Tajín, Niche pyramid</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Quiahuiztlán is on the coast on a small mountain named Bernal. It is cut into the mountain as a series of terraces. It is located very close to where Cortés founded the initial Spanish settlement of Villa Rica de la Vera-Cruz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-17"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Castillo de Teayo (Teayo Castle) is really a pyramid, whose original name was Zapotitlán. It is located on the border between Huasctec and Totonac lands. It was abandoned in the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-18"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>The largest and most important site is <a href="/wiki/El_Taj%C3%ADn" title="El Tajín">El Tajín</a>, located near the city of Papantla. The name is from the Totonac language and means “thunder,” but no one knows what the true name of this city was. It is also unknown if the Totonac built it, but since they have dominated the region for centuries, they lay claim to it.The city developed from the end of the Classic period and the beginning of the Post Classic period, between 800 and 1150 C.E. It is divided into five zones, the Plaza del Arroyo Group, the Central Zone, the Gran Xicalcoliuhqui, Tajín Chico and the Column Complex. Its signature building is the Pyramid of the Niches, named after the 365 niches built into the levels of the structure. The site has a large number of Mesoamerican ball game courts, one with details reliefs showing the beheading of a ballplayer and his role in the afterlife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-19"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Tres Zapotes site is located the community of the same name. Covering 1.5 hectares, the main building has a square base, which is surrounded by gardens and trees. The most important find from his is Stele “C” which is on display at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Museo_Nacional_de_Antropolog%C3%ADa" title="Museo Nacional de Antropología">Museo Nacional de Antropología</a> in Mexico City.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-20"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>El Pital is a site in the municipality of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mart%C3%ADnez_de_la_Torre,_Veracruz" title="Martínez de la Torre, Veracruz">Martínez de la Torre</a>. It consists of a mound with a pyramid base and stairs on the east side.The site’s culture is considered to be a link between the coastal and highland cultures of the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-21"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>Los Idolos is a site in the municipal city of <a href="/wiki/Misantla" title="Misantla">Misantla</a>, and was an important ceremonial site for the Totonacapan region. It consists of four rectangular patios linked by platforms and flat-topped mounds. Many of the structures are decorated with smooth river stone, thought to have come from the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Misantla_River&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Misantla River (page does not exist)">Misantla River</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-22"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Centro Ceremonial Cuajilote is located on the <a href="/wiki/Bobos_River_(Mexico)" title="Bobos River (Mexico)">Bobos River</a>. It consists of a large plaza 400 meters long lined with structures. In the center of the plaza there are three shrines, one of which contains phallic figures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atractcul_35-23"><a href="#cite_note-atractcul-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Veracruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Veracruz became a state in 1824. Its government is headed by a governor, who is elected to a single term of six years. Members of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unicameral" title="Unicameral">unicameral</a> legislature, the State Congress, are elected to three-year terms. The state is divided into 212 local governmental units called <a href="/wiki/Municipalities_of_Mexico" title="Municipalities of Mexico">municipios (municipalities)</a>, each of which is headquartered in a prominent city, town, or village.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brittanica_14-9"><a href="#cite_note-brittanica-14">[14]</a></sup> The newest of these are the municipalities of <a href="/wiki/San_Rafael,_Veracruz" title="San Rafael, Veracruz">San Rafael</a> and <a href="/wiki/Santiago_Sochiapan" title="Santiago Sochiapan">Santiago Sochiapan</a> which were created in 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gobierno_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-gobierno-51">[51]</a></sup> These municipalities are grouped into regions called Huasteca Alta (with ten municipalities), Huasteca Baja (with 23 municipalities), Totonac (with 15 municipalities), Nautla (with 11 municipalities), Capital (with 33 municipalities), Sotavento (with 12 municipalities), De las Montañas (with 57 municipalities), Papaloapan (with 22 municipalities), De los Tuxtlas (with four municipalities) and Olmeca (with 25 municipalities) .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-regionalizacion_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-regionalizacion-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>Freedom of the Press violations</b><br/>
According to many journalists' organizations, Veracruz is one of the most dangerous places for journalists especially after governor <a href="/wiki/Javier_Duarte_de_Ochoa" title="Javier Duarte de Ochoa">Javier Duarte de Ochoa</a> came to power in December 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Infrastructure">Infrastructure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Veracruz&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Infrastructure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The road system in the state contains 16,039 km (9,966.2 mi), representing 5.1% of the roads nationwide. For each 100 km<sup>2</sup> (38.6 sq mi) of territory, there are 22 km (13.7 mi) of roads. 3,144.5 km (1,953.9 mi) are part of the federal highway system. State roads comprise 2,176 km (1,352.1 mi) with the rest maintained by local authorities. There are over 3,000 km (1,864.1 mi) of rural roads, but only 71.5 km (44.4 mi) are paved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-infraes_44-3"><a href="#cite_note-infraes-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>The state contains 1,675.3 km (1,041.0 mi) of railway. Most of this is conceded by the federal government to private companies, with strategic stretches maintained directly by the government. Some of the private companies include <a href="/wiki/Kansas_City_Southern_de_M%C3%A9xico" title="Kansas City Southern de México">Kansas City Southern de México</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ferrosur" title="Ferrosur">Ferrosur</a>. These lines are used almost exclusively for the transportation of freight, which in 1999 added up to 37 million tons. Three rail lines serve the port of Veracruz exclusively. One is dedicated to the port of Coatzacalcos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-infraes_44-4"><a href="#cite_note-infraes-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>The ports of Veracruz are Tuxpan, Veracruz, Coatzacoalcos, Pajaritos, Minatitlán-Nanchital, Tecolutla, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nautla" title="Nautla">Nautla</a>, Alvarado and Tlacotalpan. The first three are the ports for heavy cargo ships, with Veracruz the most important of the three. The others are small ports for small ships, fishing boats and tourism. All ports are operated privately with the exception of Pajaritos, which is operated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PEMEX" title="PEMEX">PEMEX</a>. Port traffic in Veracruz account for 10% of all commercial traffic in the country, 23.4% of the port traffic of Mexico and 21% of all port traffic in the Gulf of Mexico and Caribbean. Goods imported through the state reach 16 out of Mexico’s 31 states plus Mexico City. The port of Veracruz alone handles over 12 million tons of freight per year. Coatzacoalcos is important for its handling of petroleum products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-infraes_44-5"><a href="#cite_note-infraes-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>The state contains three major airports. “El Tajín” in Tihuatlán serving Poza Rica and “Canticas” in Minatitlán provide national service. “Heriberto Jara Corona” in the city of Veracruz provides national and international service. There are also 31 smaller regional airfields in municipalities such as Acayucán, <a href="/wiki/Cazones_de_Herrera" title="Cazones de Herrera">Cazones de Herrera</a>, Córdoba, <a href="/wiki/Cuitl%C3%A1huac,_Veracruz" title="Cuitláhuac, Veracruz">Cuitlahuac</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ju%C3%A1n_Rodr%C3%ADguez_Clara&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Juán Rodríguez Clara (page does not exist)">Juán Rodríguez Clara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ozuluama" title="Ozuluama">Ozuluama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plat%C3%B3n_S%C3%A1nchez" title="Platón Sánchez">Platón Sánchez</a>, <a href="/wiki/Playa_Vicente" title="Playa Vicente">Playa Vicente</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soconusco" title="Soconusco">Soconusco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tamal%C3%ADn" title="Tamalín">Tamalín</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tamiahua" title="Tamiahua">Tamiahua</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tecolutla" title="Tecolutla">Tecolutla</a>, <a href="/wiki/Temapache" title="Temapache">Temapache</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tempoal_de_S%C3%A1nchez,_Veracruz" title="Tempoal de Sánchez, Veracruz">Tempoal</a> and Tierra Blanca.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-infraes_44-6"><a href="#cite_note-infraes-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are 59 local newspapers and 40 magazines published in the state. These include the Diario de Xalapa, El Dictamen, El Sol del Centro and la Opinión de Minatitlán. There are 202 radio stations (57 AM, 35 FM). Most are commercial or private but some are operated by non-profits and governmental agencies. There are 22 television stations; two channels are local, and the rest are repeaters from national broadcasters. Five companies provide cable and satellite television. <a href="/wiki/Telmex" title="Telmex">Telmex</a> controls over 75% of the telephone service in the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-infraes_44-7"><a href="#cite_note-infraes-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
